ƿ &wynn; 01BF LatExtB LATIN LETTER WYNN V Variant Letter Forms  
Ƿ &WYNN; 01F7 LatExtB LATIN CAPITAL LETTER WYNN V Variant Letter Forms The character WYNN was originally a Runic letter in the common Germanic runic alphabet, ᚹ (16B9), called wynn in the Anglo-Saxon Rune poem. It was borrowed into the Latin alphabet in England to denote /w/ and is usually rendered with ‹w› in simplified transcriptions.
ꝩ &vins; A769 LatExtD LATIN SMALL LETTER VEND V Variant Letter Forms  
Ꝩ &Vins; A768 LatExtD LATIN CAPITAL LETTER VEND V Variant Letter Forms The Medieval Nordic form of this character is based on the Old English character WYNN (01BF), but it is open at the top, and often has a dot above to distinguish it from ‹y›. It was used to denote /v/ and is always rendered with ‹v› in simplified transcriptions. Since it was borrowed from Old English, it is usually referred to as an Insular character, although it ultimately derives from the Runic alphabet. However, at the time of borrowing in the 11th century, the runic character ᚹ (16B9) had been replaced by ᚢ (16A2) in the younger futhark.

Ꝡ &YYlig; A760 LatExtD LATIN CAPITAL LETTER VY Y Ligatures The ‘y+y’ letter (ligature) was introduced by the Icelander Jón Ólafsson from Grunnavík in the 18th century. Cf. . . 002E BasLat FULL STOP Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters The full stop is often used as a mark of abbreviation, typically in suspended (truncated) words. If an encoder wants to make a distinction between the full stop as a punctuation mark and as an abbreviation mark, we recommend that the same character, 002E FULL STOP, should be used in both cases, but that the abbreviation mark is specified by markup, e.g. by the element in the Text Encoding Initiative (TEI) P5 Guidelines.
; ; 003B BasLat SEMICOLON Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters In Medieval script, a sign looking like the semicolon was commonly used as an abbreviation for ‘eð’ or ‘ed’, cf. LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN SEMICOLON at F1AC on p. 136 below. If an encoder wants to use 003B SEMICOLON for the abbreviation sign, we recommend that this usage should be specified by markup, e.g. by the element in the Text Encoding Initiative (TEI) P5 Guidelines.
& & 0026 BasLat AMPERSAND Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters In Medieval primary sources, the ampersand was used as an abbreviation for the conjunction ‘and’ (Latin ‘et’, Medieval Nordic ‘ok’, etc.), in which case it can be specified by markup, e.g. by the element in the Text Encoding Initiative (TEI) P5 Guidelines.
ß ß 00DF Lat1Suppl LATIN SMALL LETTER SHARP S Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters In Medieval sources, the “sharp s” can also be used as an abbreviation character, in which case it may be specified by markup, e.g. by the element.
Θ Θ 0398 Gk & Co GREEK CAPITAL LETTER THETA Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
θ θ 03B8 Gk & Co GREEK SMALL LETTER THETA Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters The theta characters are sometimes used as an abbreviation sign for obiit ‘he/she died’, but we recommend using the alternative obiit pair of characters below. See also the introduction in Ernst W.E. Hübner, Exempla Scripturae Epigraphicae Latinae, Berlin: Reimer 1885 (rpt. Berlin: de Gruyter, 1979), in which he states that the theta should be kept apart from the obiit sign (barred ‘o’).
ꝋ &obiit; A74B LatExtD LATIN SMALL LETTER O WITH LONG STROKE OVERLAY Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
Ꝋ &OBIIT; A74A LatExtD LATIN CAPITAL LETTER O WITH LONG STROKE OVERLAY Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters As pointed out above, the character 03B8 GREEK SMALL LETTER THETA in Greek and Coptic is sometimes used for rendering this abbreviation, but we recommend having a separate obiit character.
⁊ &et; 204A GenPunct LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN SMALL ET Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters The Unicode descriptive name for this character is TIRONIAN SIGN ET. It was frequently used in Medieval sources, and is still used in Modern Irish.
 &etslash; F158 PUA-4 LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN SMALL ET WITH STROKE Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
 &ET; F142 PUA-4 LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN CAPITAL ET Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
 &ETslash; F1A7 PUA-4 LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN CAPITAL ET WITH STROKE Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
ʼ &apomod; 02BC SpModLet MODIFIER LETTER APOSTROPHE Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters The ordinary apostrophe, 0027 APOSTROPHE, has been listed in section 5 below. We recommend that the character 02BC should be used to encode ab­breviations, and that 0027 should be used in all other contexts.
≈ &esse; 2248 MathOp LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN ESSE Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters The Unicode descriptive name for this character is ALMOST EQUAL TO.
∻ &est; 223B MathOp LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN EST Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters The Unicode descriptive name for this character is HOMOTHETIC.
ꝯ &condes; A76F LatExtD LATIN SMALL LETTER CON Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
Ꝯ &CONdes; A76E LatExtD LATIN CAPITAL LETTER CON Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters Another form of the ‘con’ abbreviation character looks like a reversed ‘C’. This character, which also was used as a Roman numeral and as a Claudian letter, antisigma, has been listed under the character ‘C’ above.
ꜿ &condot; A73F LatExtD LATIN SMALL LETTER REVERSED C WITH DOT Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
Ꜿ &CONdot; A73E LatExtD LATIN CAPITAL LETTER REVERSED C WITH DOT Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
 &usbase; F1A6 PUA-4 LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN SPACING BASE-LINE US Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters This character is positioned on the base line, but not with full height.
 &USbase; F1A5 PUA-4 LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN SPACING BASE-LINE CAPITAL US Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters This character is positioned on the base line and has capital form (full height).
ꝰ &usmod; A770 LatExtD MODIFIER LETTER US Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters This character is positioned above the base line, the top aligning with the top of ascenders.
ꝝ &rum; A75D LatExtD LATIN SMALL LETTER RUM ROTUNDA Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
Ꝝ &RUM; A75C LatExtD LATIN CAPITAL LETTER RUM ROTUNDA Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
 &de; F159 PUA-4 LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN SMALL DE Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
ꝭ &is; A76D LatExtD LATIN SMALL LETTER IS Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
Ꝭ &IS; A76C LatExtD LATIN CAPITAL LETTER IS Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
ꝸ &sstrok; A778 LatExtD LATIN SMALL LETTER UM Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters This character can also be used for the ‘soldi’ sign (see p. 159 below)
ꝫ &etfin; A76B LatExtD LATIN SMALL LETTER ET Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
Ꝫ &ETfin; A76A LatExtD LATIN CAPITAL LETTER ET Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters The two characters above are used as suspension marks in a number of contexts, e.g. for ‘et’ in ‘videlicet’, for ‘us’ in ‘quibus’, ‘omnibus’, for ‘ue’ in ‘ne­que’, ‘cumque’, for ‘m’ in ‘nam’, ‘omnem’, for ‘est‘ in ‘prodest’, ‘interest’, etc. In the paleographical literature, it is usually described as “a sign looking like the number 3”. It is difficult to find a wholly suitable name; the name proposed here takes the abbreviation of ‘et’ in ‘videlicet’ to be prototypical.
 &sem; F1AC PUA-4 LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN SEMICOLON Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters The character 003B SEMICOLON can be used for the frequent abbreviation sign of similar shape. However, some encoders might want to have a separate character, in which case we recommend using F1AC LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN SEMICOLON. Neither character should be unified with F1EA PUNCTUS VERSUS, which is a punctuation mark (see p. 147 below).
ᚠ &fMedrun; 16A0 Run RUNIC MEDIEVAL LETTER F Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters Runes are alphabetical signs in their own right, but in Medieval Nordic manuscripts written in the Latin alphabet they function as abbreviation characters, e.g. the ‘f’ rune, meaning ‘fé’ (cattle and/or goods). The Unicode descriptive name for this character is RUNIC LETTER FEHU FEOH FE F, but here a simpler name has been given, RUNIC MEDIEVAL LETTER F.
ᛘ &mMedrun; 16D8 Run RUNIC MEDIEVAL LETTER M Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters In Medieval Nordic manuscripts written in the Latin alphabet, the ‘m’ rune serves as an abbreviation for ‘maðr’ (man). The Unicode descriptive name for this character is RUNIC LETTER LONG-BRANCH-MADR M, but here a simpler name has been given, RUNIC MEDIEVAL LETTER M.
℔ &lbbar; 2114 LettSymb L B BAR SYMBOL Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters Used as an abbreviation for the weight unity ‘pound’. For variants, see section 7 Symbols below.

⸌ &luslst; 2E0C SupplPunct LEFT UPPER SLANTED STROKE Punctuation Vertical and Oblique Bars The Unicode descriptive name is LEFT RAISED OMISSION BRACKET, but the more general LEFT UPPER SLANTED STROKE has been chosen here. In Medieval Nordic editing, it is typically used to indicate the beginning of text added above the line.
⸍ &ruslst; 2E0D SupplPunct RIGHT UPPER SLANTED STROKE Punctuation Vertical and Oblique Bars The Unicode descriptive name is RIGHT RAISED OMISSION BRACKET, but the more general RIGHT UPPER SLANTED STROKE has been chosen here. In Medieval Nordic editing, it is typically used to indicate the end of text added above the line.
⸜ &rlslst; 2E1C SupplPunct RIGHT LOWER SLANTED STROKE Punctuation Vertical and Oblique Bars The Unicode descriptive name is LEFT LOW PARAPHRASE BRACKET, but the more general name RIGHT LOWER SLANTED STROKE has been chosen here. In Medieval Nordic editing, it is typically used to indicate the end of text added in the margin and is for this reason called ‘RIGHT’ (by analogy with e.g. 0029 RIGHT PARENTHESIS).
⸝ &llslst; 2E1D SupplPunct LEFT LOWER SLANTED STROKE Punctuation Vertical and Oblique Bars The Unicode descriptive name is RIGHT LOW PARAPHRASE BRACKET, but the more general name LEFT LOWER SLANTED STROKE has been chosen here. In Medieval Nordic editing, it is typically used to indicate the beginning of text added in the margin and is for this reason called ‘LEFT’ (by analogy with e.g. 0028 LEFT PARENTHESIS).

⟩ &rangb; 27E9 MiMaSymbA RIGHT ANGLE BRACKET Punctuation Angles The Unicode descriptive name is MATHEMATICAL RIGHT ANGLE BRACKET, but the word ‘MATHEMATICAL’ has been left out here. This bracket is typically used to indicate the end of an editorial addition (supplied text).
 &hidot; F1F8 PUA-8 DISTINCTIO Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Described by the grammarian Donatus, who distinguishes between three positions of the dot: on the baseline (to be unified with 002E FULL STOP), middle height (to be unified with 00B7 MIDDLE DOT) and the high dot, shown here. See also Isidore of Sevilla, Etymologiae I 20. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, p. 13, says that the distinctio was used to indicate “a final pause, after a periodus, or where the sententia is completed”.
 &posit; F1E2 PUA-8 COMMA POSITURA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks The positura is similar to COMMA 002C, but should be kept apart from this punctuation mark due to a different and more specialised usage. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301 and 306.
 &ductsimpl; F1E3 PUA-8 HIGH COMMA POSITURA (SIMPLEX DUCTUS) Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Similar form as the positura, but positioned above the ‘x’ height. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301 and 307.
 &punctvers; F1EA PUA-8 PUNCTUS VERSUS Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Similar in shape to the semicolon (003B), but should be kept apart from this mark. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301, 306.
 &punctposit; F1E4 PUA-8 PUNCTUS WITH COMMA POSITURA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301 and 306.
 &colmidcomposit; F1E5 PUA-8 COLON WITH MIDDLE COMMA POSITURA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301 and 306.
 &bidotscomposit; F1F2 PUA-8 TWO DOTS OVER COMMA POSITURA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Attested in Old Icelandic, e.g. in Holm perg 15 B 4to (see Hreinn Benediktsson, Early Icelandic Script, Reykjavík 1965, pl. 8:14) and in GKS 2365 4to (Codex Regius of the Eddic poems).
 &tridotscomposit; F1E6 PUA-8 THREE DOTS WITH COMMA POSITURA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks This punctuation mark looks like an upwards-poiting triangle of dots with a comma between (and below) the two lower dots. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301 and 307. See also 2234 above in Mathematical Operators.
 &punctelev; F161 PUA-8 PUNCTUS ELEVATUS Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks This form of the punctus elevatus is typically found in English manuscripts and is sometimes seen as the default form. Sometimes called ‘tick and point’, e.g. by N.R. Ker, English Manuscripts in the Century after the Norman Conquest, Oxford, 1960, p. 46.
 &punctelevdiag; F1F0 PUA-8 PUNCTUS ELEVATUS DIAGONAL STROKE Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Variant form of the punctus elevatus.
 &punctelevhiback; F1FA PUA-8 PUNCTUS ELEVATUS WITH HIGH BACK Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Variant form of the punctus elevatus found in manuscripts in the Low countries, eastern France and the Rhineland.
 &punctelevhack; F1FB PUA-8 PUNCTUS ELEVATUS WITH ONSET Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Variant form of the punctus elevatus found in manuscripts from southern Germany.
 &punctflex; F1F5 PUA-8 PUNCTUS FLEXUS Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Looks like the digit 7 with a dot below, sometimes referred to as ‘seven and point’ (e.g. by N.R. Ker, English Manuscripts in the Century after the Norman Conquest, Oxford, 1960, p. 47). Also called PUNCTUS CIRCUMFLEXUS. Used to mark sentence-medial pauses, especially in liturgical texts where the pitch of the voice drops. Cf. also Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301, 306. Note that the shape in Parkes’ book looks more like an open ‘a’ than ‘7’, but this is basically a question of variance.
 &punctexclam; F1E7 PUA-8 PUNCTUS EXCLAMATIVUS Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Early form with two dots below each other and a diagonal stroke on top of them. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, p. 301.
 &punctinter; F160 PUA-8 PUNCTUS INTERROGATIVUS Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks  
 &punctintertilde; F1E8 PUA-8 PUNCTUS INTERROGATIVUS HORIZONTAL TILDE Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks This is a variant of the question mark where the swash (tilde) is horizontal. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, p. 301.
 &punctinterlemn; F1F1 PUA-8 PUNCTUS INTERROGATIVUS LEMNISKATE FORM Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Attested in Old Icelandic, but very marginal. For examples, see Holm perg 15 B 4to (cf. Hreinn Benediktsson, Early Icelandic Script, Reykjavík 1965, pl. 8:13 and 8:19).
⸮ &punctpercont; 2E2E SupplPunct REVERSED QUESTION MARK Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Looks like a horizontally flipped (i.e. reversed) modern question mark, called PUNCTUS PERCONTATIVUS. Used in late texts to mark the end of a question that does not require an answer (i.e. a rhetorical question), as opposed to the PUNCTUS INTERROGATIVUS.
 &wavylin; F1F9 PUA-8 WAVY LINE Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Frequently used in English manuscripts in the 12th century in the form of a wavy line or sometimes a straight line. Placed slightly above the base line.
 &medcom; F1E0 PUA-8 MEDIEVAL COMMA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, p. 301. According to Parkes, this mark “appears most frequently in the work of fourteenth-century Italian scribes” (p. 303).
 ¶g; F1E1 PUA-8 PARAGRAPHUS Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 12, 43, 305.
 &renvoi; F1EC PUA-8 SIGNE DE RENVOI Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Looks like two dots over a single dot. Used to connect a marginal note to a place in the text. Looks similar to TWO DOTS OVER ONE DOT PUNCTUATION below, but has a different usage and the dots are more widely spaced.
⸪ &tridotsdownw; 2E2A SupplPunct TWO DOTS OVER ONE DOT PUNCTUATION Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Similar to 2235 BECAUSE in Mathematical Operators, but should be recognised as a separate punctuation character and given its own code point.
⸫ &tridotsupw; 2E2B SupplPunct ONE DOT OVER TWO DOTS PUNCTUATION Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Similar to 2234 THEREFORE in Mathematical Operators, but should be recognised as a separate punctuation character and given its own code point.
⸬ &quaddots; 2E2C SupplPunct FOUR DOTS PUNCTUATION Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Similar to 2237 PROPORTION in Mathematical Operators, but should be recognised as a separate punctuation character and given its own code point.
⸭ &fivedots; 2E2D SupplPunct FIVE DOT MARK Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks  
 &virgsusp; F1F4 PUA-8 VIRGULA SUSPENSIVA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Looks like a solidus with a dot in the middle. Used to mark a very brief pause or hesitation in the text. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, p. 307.
 &virgmin; F1F7 PUA-8 SHORT VIRGULA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Looks like a virgule, but confined within the ‘x’ height (like a half-height slash), e.g. ‘x  x’. Attested in late paper manuscripts of Medieval Nordic verse and not to be unified with the ordinary comma.
⋗ &dipledot; 22D7 MathOp DIPLE WITH DOT Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks The Unicode name for this character is GREATER-THAN WITH DOT, but it can also be used as a punctuation mark and is therefore given another name here, DIPLE WITH DOT. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301 and 303.

& & 0026 BasLat AMPERSAND Symbols General Symbols In SGML/XML encoded documents, the ampersand is the opening character of an entity name, as exemplified throughout this recommendation. To avoid confusion, the ampersand as such can be encoded with the entity ‘&’. In Medieval primary sources, the ampersand was used as an abbreviation for the conjunction ‘and’ (Latin ‘et’, Medieval Nordic ‘ok’, etc.), in which case it can be specified by markup, e.g. by the element in the Text Encoding Initiative (TEI) P5 Guidelines.

 &metranc; F70A PUA-10 METRICAL SYMBOL ANCEPS Metrical Characters Precomposed Characters As stated above, this character can be encoded with 00D7 MULTIPLICATION SIGN and the entity name ‘×’ (p. 164 above). We recommend that this code point should be used hereafter. However, for those who would like to continue using the complete set of metrical symbols in the Private Use Area, we recommend that the character F70A should be given the entity name ‘&metranc;’ and the descriptive name METRICAL SYMBOL ANCEPS.

℣ &Vslstrok; 2123 LettSymb VERSICLE     For design reasons, A75E LATIN CAPITAL LETTER V WITH DIAGONAL STROKE and 2123 VERSICLE have identical glyphs in the font used here, Andron Scriptor Web. ƿ &wynn; 01BF LatExtB LATIN LETTER WYNN V Variant Letter Forms  
Ƿ &WYNN; 01F7 LatExtB LATIN CAPITAL LETTER WYNN V Variant Letter Forms The character WYNN was originally a Runic letter in the common Germanic runic alphabet, ᚹ (16B9), called wynn in the Anglo-Saxon Rune poem. It was borrowed into the Latin alphabet in England to denote /w/ and is usually rendered with ‹w› in simplified transcriptions.
ꝩ &vins; A769 LatExtD LATIN SMALL LETTER VEND V Variant Letter Forms  
Ꝩ &Vins; A768 LatExtD LATIN CAPITAL LETTER VEND V Variant Letter Forms distinguish it from ‹y›. It was used to denote /v/ and is always rendered with ‹v› in simplified transcriptions. Since it was borrowed from Old English, it is usually referred to as an Insular character, although it ultimately derives from the Runic alphabet. However, at the time of borrowing in the 11th century, the runic character ᚹ (16B9) had been replaced by ᚢ (16A2) in the younger futhark.

Ꝡ &YYlig; A760 LatExtD LATIN CAPITAL LETTER VY Y Ligatures The ‘y+y’ letter (ligature) was introduced by the Icelander Jón Ólafsson from Grunnavík in the 18th century. Cf. . . 002E BasLat FULL STOP Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters The full stop is often used as a mark of abbreviation, typically in suspended (truncated) words. If an encoder wants to make a distinction between the full stop as a punctuation mark and as an abbreviation mark, we recommend that the same character, 002E FULL STOP, should be used in both cases, but that the abbreviation mark is specified by markup, e.g. by the element in the Text Encoding Initiative (TEI) P5 Guidelines.
; ; 003B BasLat SEMICOLON Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters In Medieval script, a sign looking like the semicolon was commonly used as an abbreviation for ‘eð’ or ‘ed’, cf. LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN SEMICOLON at F1AC on p. 136 below. If an encoder wants to use 003B SEMICOLON for the abbreviation sign, we recommend that this usage should be specified by markup, e.g. by the element in the Text Encoding Initiative (TEI) P5 Guidelines.
& & 26 BasLat AMPERSAND Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters In Medieval primary sources, the ampersand was used as an abbreviation for the conjunction ‘and’ (Latin ‘et’, Medieval Nordic ‘ok’, etc.), in which case it can be specified by markup, e.g. by the element in the Text Encoding Initiative (TEI) P5 Guidelines.
ß ß 00DF Lat1Suppl LATIN SMALL LETTER SHARP S Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters In Medieval sources, the “sharp s” can also be used as an abbreviation character, in which case it may be specified by markup, e.g. by the element.
Θ Θ 398 Gk & Co GREEK CAPITAL LETTER THETA Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
θ θ 03B8 Gk & Co GREEK SMALL LETTER THETA Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters The theta characters are sometimes used as an abbreviation sign for obiit ‘he/she died’, but we recommend using the alternative obiit pair of characters below. See also the introduction in Ernst W.E. Hübner, Exempla Scripturae Epigraphicae Latinae, Berlin: Reimer 1885 (rpt. Berlin: de Gruyter, 1979), in which he states that the theta should be kept apart from the obiit sign (barred ‘o’).
ꝋ &obiit; A74B LatExtD LATIN SMALL LETTER O WITH LONG STROKE OVERLAY Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
Ꝋ &OBIIT; A74A LatExtD LATIN CAPITAL LETTER O WITH LONG STROKE OVERLAY Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters As pointed out above, the character 03B8 GREEK SMALL LETTER THETA in Greek and Coptic is sometimes used for rendering this abbreviation, but we recommend having a separate obiit character.
⁊ &et; 204A GenPunct LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN SMALL ET Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters The Unicode descriptive name for this character is TIRONIAN SIGN ET. It was frequently used in Medieval sources, and is still used in Modern Irish.

We recommend that the character 02BC should be used to encode ab­breviations, and that 0027 should be used in all other contexts.

Ꝯ &CONdes; A76E LatExtD LATIN CAPITAL LETTER CON Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters Another form of the ‘con’ abbreviation character looks like a reversed ‘C’. This character, which also was used as a Roman numeral and as a Claudian letter, antisigma, has been listed under the character ‘C’ above.

 &usbase; F1A6 PUA-4 LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN SPACING BASE-LINE US Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters This character is positioned on the base line, but not with full height.

ꝸ &sstrok; A778 LatExtD LATIN SMALL LETTER UM Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters This character can also be used for the ‘soldi’ sign (see p. 159 below).
ꝫ &etfin; A76B LatExtD LATIN SMALL LETTER ET Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters  
Ꝫ &ETfin; A76A LatExtD LATIN CAPITAL LETTER ET Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters The two characters above are used as suspension marks in a number of contexts, e.g. for ‘et’ in ‘videlicet’, for ‘us’ in ‘quibus’, ‘omnibus’, for ‘ue’ in ‘ne­que’, ‘cumque’, for ‘m’ in ‘nam’, ‘omnem’, for ‘est‘ in ‘prodest’, ‘interest’, etc. In the paleographical literature, it is usually described as “a sign looking like the number 3”. It is difficult to find a wholly suitable name; the name proposed here takes the abbreviation of ‘et’ in ‘videlicet’ to be prototypical.
 &sem; F1AC PUA-4 LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN SEMICOLON Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters The character 003B SEMICOLON can be used for the frequent abbreviation sign of similar shape. However, some encoders might want to have a separate character, in which case we recommend using F1AC LATIN ABBREVIATION SIGN SEMICOLON. Neither character should be unified with F1EA PUNCTUS VERSUS, which is a punctuation mark (see p. 147 below).
ᚠ &fMedrun; 16A0 Run RUNIC MEDIEVAL LETTER F Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters Runes are alphabetical signs in their own right, but in Medieval Nordic manuscripts written in the Latin alphabet they function as abbreviation characters, e.g. the ‘f’ rune, meaning ‘fé’ (cattle and/or goods). The Unicode descriptive name for this character is RUNIC LETTER FEHU FEOH FE F, but here a simpler name has been given, RUNIC MEDIEVAL LETTER F.
ᛘ &mMedrun; 16D8 Run RUNIC MEDIEVAL LETTER M Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters In Medieval Nordic manuscripts written in the Latin alphabet, the ‘m’ rune serves as an abbreviation for ‘maðr’ (man). The Unicode descriptive name for this character is RUNIC LETTER LONG-BRANCH-MADR M, but here a simpler name has been given, RUNIC MEDIEVAL LETTER M.
℔ &lbbar; 2114 LettSymb L B BAR SYMBOL Spacing and Modifying Characters Abbreviation Characters Used as an abbreviation for the weight unity ‘pound’. For variants, see section 7 Symbols below.

⸌ &luslst; 2E0C SupplPunct LEFT UPPER SLANTED STROKE Punctuation Vertical and Oblique Bars The Unicode descriptive name is LEFT RAISED OMISSION BRACKET, but the more general LEFT UPPER SLANTED STROKE has been chosen here. In Medieval Nordic editing, it is typically used to indicate the beginning of text added above the line.
⸍ &ruslst; 2E0D SupplPunct RIGHT UPPER SLANTED STROKE Punctuation Vertical and Oblique Bars The Unicode descriptive name is RIGHT RAISED OMISSION BRACKET, but the more general RIGHT UPPER SLANTED STROKE has been chosen here. In Medieval Nordic editing, it is typically used to indicate the end of text added above the line.
⸜ &rlslst; 2E1C SupplPunct RIGHT LOWER SLANTED STROKE Punctuation Vertical and Oblique Bars The Unicode descriptive name is LEFT LOW PARAPHRASE BRACKET, but the more general name RIGHT LOWER SLANTED STROKE has been chosen here. In Medieval Nordic editing, it is typically used to indicate the end of text added in the margin and is for this reason called ‘RIGHT’ (by analogy with e.g. 0029 RIGHT PARENTHESIS).
⸝ &llslst; 2E1D SupplPunct LEFT LOWER SLANTED STROKE Punctuation Vertical and Oblique Bars The Unicode descriptive name is RIGHT LOW PARAPHRASE BRACKET, but the more general name LEFT LOWER SLANTED STROKE has been chosen here. In Medieval Nordic editing, it is typically used to indicate the beginning of text added in the margin and is for this reason called ‘LEFT’ (by analogy with e.g. 0028 LEFT PARENTHESIS).

⟩ &rangb; 2.70E+10 MiMaSymbA RIGHT ANGLE BRACKET Punctuation Angles The Unicode descriptive name is MATHEMATICAL RIGHT ANGLE BRACKET, but the word ‘MATHEMATICAL’ has been left out here. This bracket is typically used to indicate the end of an editorial addition (supplied text).
 &hidot; F1F8 PUA-8 DISTINCTIO Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Described by the grammarian Donatus, who distinguishes between three positions of the dot: on the baseline (to be unified with 002E FULL STOP), middle height (to be unified with 00B7 MIDDLE DOT) and the high dot, shown here. See also Isidore of Sevilla, Etymologiae I 20. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, p. 13, says that the distinctio was used to indicate “a final pause, after a periodus, or where the sententia is completed”.
 &posit; F1E2 PUA-8 COMMA POSITURA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks The positura is similar to COMMA 002C, but should be kept apart from this punctuation mark due to a different and more specialised usage. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301 and 306.
 &ductsimpl; F1E3 PUA-8 HIGH COMMA POSITURA (SIMPLEX DUCTUS) Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Similar form as the positura, but positioned above the ‘x’ height. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301 and 307.
 &punctvers; F1EA PUA-8 PUNCTUS VERSUS Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Similar in shape to the semicolon (003B), but should be kept apart from this mark. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301, 306.
 &punctposit; F1E4 PUA-8 PUNCTUS WITH COMMA POSITURA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301 and 306.
 &colmidcomposit; F1E5 PUA-8 COLON WITH MIDDLE COMMA POSITURA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301 and 306.
 &bidotscomposit; F1F2 PUA-8 TWO DOTS OVER COMMA POSITURA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Attested in Old Icelandic, e.g. in Holm perg 15 B 4to (see Hreinn Benediktsson, Early Icelandic Script, Reykjavík 1965, pl. 8:14) and in GKS 2365 4to (Codex Regius of the Eddic poems).
 &tridotscomposit; F1E6 PUA-8 THREE DOTS WITH COMMA POSITURA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks This punctuation mark looks like an upwards-poiting triangle of dots with a comma between (and below) the two lower dots. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301 and 307. See also 2234 above in Mathematical Operators.
 &punctelev; F161 PUA-8 PUNCTUS ELEVATUS Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks This form of the punctus elevatus is typically found in English manuscripts and is sometimes seen as the default form. Sometimes called ‘tick and point’, e.g. by N.R. Ker, English Manuscripts in the Century after the Norman Conquest, Oxford, 1960, p. 46.
 &punctelevdiag; F1F0 PUA-8 PUNCTUS ELEVATUS DIAGONAL STROKE Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Variant form of the punctus elevatus.
 &punctelevhiback; F1FA PUA-8 PUNCTUS ELEVATUS WITH HIGH BACK Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Variant form of the punctus elevatus found in manuscripts in the Low countries, eastern France and the Rhineland.
 &punctelevhack; F1FB PUA-8 PUNCTUS ELEVATUS WITH ONSET Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Variant form of the punctus elevatus found in manuscripts from southern Germany.
 &punctflex; F1F5 PUA-8 PUNCTUS FLEXUS Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Looks like the digit 7 with a dot below, sometimes referred to as ‘seven and point’ (e.g. by N.R. Ker, English Manuscripts in the Century after the Norman Conquest, Oxford, 1960, p. 47). Also called PUNCTUS CIRCUMFLEXUS. Used to mark sentence-medial pauses, especially in liturgical texts where the pitch of the voice drops. Cf. also Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301, 306. Note that the shape in Parkes’ book looks more like an open ‘a’ than ‘7’, but this is basically a question of variance.
 &punctexclam; F1E7 PUA-8 PUNCTUS EXCLAMATIVUS Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Early form with two dots below each other and a diagonal stroke on top of them. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, p. 301.
 &punctinter; F160 PUA-8 PUNCTUS INTERROGATIVUS Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks  
 &punctintertilde; F1E8 PUA-8 PUNCTUS INTERROGATIVUS HORIZONTAL TILDE Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks This is a variant of the question mark where the swash (tilde) is horizontal. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, p. 301.
 &punctinterlemn; F1F1 PUA-8 PUNCTUS INTERROGATIVUS LEMNISKATE FORM Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Attested in Old Icelandic, but very marginal. For examples, see Holm perg 15 B 4to (cf. Hreinn Benediktsson, Early Icelandic Script, Reykjavík 1965, pl. 8:13 and 8:19).
⸮ &punctpercont; 2E2E SupplPunct REVERSED QUESTION MARK Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Looks like a horizontally flipped (i.e. reversed) modern question mark, called PUNCTUS PERCONTATIVUS. Used in late texts to mark the end of a question that does not require an answer (i.e. a rhetorical question), as opposed to the PUNCTUS INTERROGATIVUS.
 &wavylin; F1F9 PUA-8 WAVY LINE Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Frequently used in English manuscripts in the 12th century in the form of a wavy line or sometimes a straight line. Placed slightly above the base line.
 &medcom; F1E0 PUA-8 MEDIEVAL COMMA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, p. 301. According to Parkes, this mark “appears most frequently in the work of fourteenth-century Italian scribes” (p. 303).
 ¶g; F1E1 PUA-8 PARAGRAPHUS Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 12, 43, 305.
 &renvoi; F1EC PUA-8 SIGNE DE RENVOI Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Looks like two dots over a single dot. Used to connect a marginal note to a place in the text. Looks similar to TWO DOTS OVER ONE DOT PUNCTUATION below, but has a different usage and the dots are more widely spaced.
⸪ &tridotsdownw; 2E2A SupplPunct TWO DOTS OVER ONE DOT PUNCTUATION Medieval Punctuation Marks Similar to 2235 BECAUSE in Mathematical Operators, but should be recognised as a separate punctuation character and given its own code point.  
⸫ &tridotsupw; 2E2B SupplPunct ONE DOT OVER TWO DOTS PUNCTUATION Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Similar to 2234 THEREFORE in Mathematical Operators, but should be recognised as a separate punctuation character and given its own code point.
⸬ &quaddots; 2E2C SupplPunct FOUR DOTS PUNCTUATION Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Similar to 2237 PROPORTION in Mathematical Operators, but should be recognised as a separate punctuation character and given its own code point.
⸭ &fivedots; 2E2D SupplPunct FIVE DOT MARK Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks  
 &virgsusp; F1F4 PUA-8 VIRGULA SUSPENSIVA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Looks like a solidus with a dot in the middle. Used to mark a very brief pause or hesitation in the text. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, p. 307.
 &virgmin; F1F7 PUA-8 SHORT VIRGULA Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks Looks like a virgule, but confined within the ‘x’ height (like a half-height slash), e.g. ‘x  x’. Attested in late paper manuscripts of Medieval Nordic verse and not to be unified with the ordinary comma.
⋗ &dipledot; 22D7 MathOp DIPLE WITH DOT Punctuation Medieval Punctuation Marks The Unicode name for this character is GREATER-THAN WITH DOT, but it can also be used as a punctuation mark and is therefore given another name here, DIPLE WITH DOT. Cf. Malcolm B. Parkes, Pause and Effect, Aldershot, 1992, pp. 301 and 303.

& & 0026 BasLat AMPERSAND Symbols General Symbols In SGML/XML encoded documents, the ampersand is the opening character of an entity name, as exemplified throughout this recommendation. To avoid confusion, the ampersand as such can be encoded with the entity ‘&’. In Medieval primary sources, the ampersand was used as an abbreviation for the conjunction ‘and’ (Latin ‘et’, Medieval Nordic ‘ok’, etc.), in which case it can be specified by markup, e.g. by the element in the Text Encoding Initiative (TEI) P5 Guidelines.

 &metranc; F70A PUA-10 METRICAL SYMBOL ANCEPS Metrical Characters Precomposed Characters As stated above, this character can be encoded with 00D7 MULTIPLICATION SIGN and the entity name ‘×’ (p. 164 above). We recommend that this code point should be used hereafter. However, for those who would like to continue using the complete set of metrical symbols in the Private Use Area, we recommend that the character F70A should be given the entity name ‘&metranc;’ and the descriptive name METRICAL SYMBOL ANCEPS.
